{"uri": "2024-07-430085178", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-22", "time": "18:45:57", "dateTime": "2024-07-22T18:45:57Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-22T15:38:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9882352948188782, "url": "https://www.kimt.com/news/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_02a57b39-86c2-5c80-8d1b-94b18bf6d7b1.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, but taking too many meds can cause problems\n\nSwallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. {"uri": "8239889719", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-23", "time": "17:44:12", "dateTime": "2024-07-23T17:44:12Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-23T17:43:28Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9764705896377563, "url": "https://roanoke.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_defd0955-743a-58d8-baf1-b8cad5c8f75d.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. The AP is solely responsible for all content.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at {"uri": "2024-07-429999953", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-22", "time": "16:59:03", "dateTime": "2024-07-22T16:59:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-22T15:38:54Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9686274528503418, "url": "https://www.kulr8.com/news/national/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_d43346b1-c177-54db-9aaf-2f4f9c286f86.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, but taking too many meds can cause problems\n\nSwallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. The AP is solely responsible for all content.\n\nMore from this section\n\n2024 Election Latest: Biden ends reelection bid, endorses VP Harris for Democratic nomination\n\nInternet outage latest | Airlines, businesses, border crossings hit by global tech disruption\n\nExodus United Global Signs Multi-Year Agreement to Bring Indian Films and TV shows to the U.S.", "source": {"uri": "kulr8.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "KULR-8 Local News"}, "authors": [{"uri": "carla_k_johnson@kulr8.com", "name": "Carla K. "news/Health", "wgt": 100}], "image": "https://www.chicagotribune.com/wp-content/uploads/2024/07/CTC-Be-Well-Too-Many-Pills200178758.jpg?w=640", "originalArticle": null, "storyUri": "eng-9753637", {"uri": "2024-07-430102652", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-22", "time": "19:10:44", "dateTime": "2024-07-22T19:10:44Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-22T19:08:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9607843160629272, "url": "https://www.wyff4.com/article/prescription-pills-doctor-review/61666741", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.If you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"Some drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"The nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.If your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"Ask for a prescription checkupStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.If you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.Your doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.Could my medicine be causing a problem?Swelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?Video above: VP Kamala Harris one on one: Health care costsPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.Weaning off medication takes timeSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.After two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.The 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.One of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.She listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"Be in charge of your medication listEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.Related video above: What you should pack in luggage to deal with travel emergenciesThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.For an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.___The Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. The AP is solely responsible for all content.\n\nSwallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nVideo above: VP Kamala Harris one on one: Health care costs\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nRelated video above: What you should pack in luggage to deal with travel emergencies\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\n___", "source": {"uri": "wyff4.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "WYFF4"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia - all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms - or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. "https://dims.apnews.com/dims4/default/d577337/2147483647/strip/true/crop/3120x1755+0+63/resize/1440x810!/quality/90/?url=https%3A%2F%2Fassets.apnews.com%2F%5B1%2F23%2F%2C%20-12%2C%20-48%2C%2021%2C%20-24%2C%2082%2C%20-107%2C%2034%2C%203%2C%20-112%2C%20-54%2C%200%2C%2037%2C%2050%2C%20-1%2C%20-108%2C%2010%2C%2026%2C%2069%2C%20104%2C%20-59%2C%20-88%2C%206%2C%2080%2C%2082%2C%20-70%2C%20123%2C%2071%5D%2Fde8cd02ba90d42e5b554763bf0522ba1", {"uri": "2024-07-429958602", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-22", "time": "16:14:55", "dateTime": "2024-07-22T16:14:55Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-22T11:38:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9333333373069763, "url": "https://www.usnews.com/news/health-news/articles/2024-07-22/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-whats-needed", "title": "Too Many Pills? How to Talk to Your Doctor About Reviewing What's Needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\n___\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. The AP is solely responsible for all content.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. Man who had himself banned from Iowa casinos wins jackpot at the Isle Chased out of Buchanan County, Balance music festival coming to Waterloo Interstate traffic shut down for person on bridge UPDATE: Man who died in lawnmower accident was Don Bosco teacher Teen seriously injured in Dunkerton rollover crash State: NewAldaya Lifescapes resident suffocated while trapped in bed rails Denver girls' track coach fired for unknown reason despite petition from community Name released in Hudson fatal shooting John Deere will lay off an undetermined number of salaried workers beginning next week Man arrested for knife threats at Waterloo apartment building College Wrestling: UNI receives major gift toward new training facility UPDATE: 15 year old injured in reported shooting 2024 Iowa Shrine Bowl: North squad downs South in offensive shootout High School Baseball: Johnston survives Cedar Falls in 13-inning 4A substate classic Final work session for proposed merged high school covers athletics, traffic, boundaries\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto. If you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy. \"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications? \"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety. After two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications. \"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces. She listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy. The only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it. For an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.", "source": {"uri": "hindustantimes.com", {"uri": "8239886335", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-23", "time": "17:41:59", "dateTime": "2024-07-23T17:41:59Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-23T17:40:49Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://buffalonews.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_f9259463-1f97-55a8-8b22-6a3620a15f17.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nPeople are also reading... Ex-Buffalo Sabres captain Kyle Okposo's thank-you letter to city, franchise North Tonawanda's best days seemed to be past. Now it's not only thriving - it's cool Juicy Burger Bar opening new restaurant on Hamburg waterfront The Foxglove Pub in Hamburg is open again ... and this time the owners plan to keep it that way Where to take out-of-town guests to eat around Buffalo Henry Nowak, longtime Buffalo congressman and Canisius basketball star, has died Two charged with making terrorist threats in separate Niagara County cases Officials identify woman killed in small plane crash near Youngstown Judge orders Battaglia Demolition owner to spend 6 months in jail These 10 Buffalo Bills have the most at stake as training camp begins. Take a look Erie County real estate transactions Inside the NHL: A summer look at the Sabres' cap situation, for now and into the future Josh Allen leads a revamped Bills offense in 2024. These five moments are shaping the new approach Religious group accused of cult-like tactics seeks to buy former Catholic seminary in Aurora Judge acquits Bongiovanni of one of his bribery charges\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. {"uri": "8239881577", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-23", "time": "17:37:29", "dateTime": "2024-07-23T17:37:29Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-23T17:36:53Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9058823585510254, "url": "https://richmond.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_ba74f535-1b28-5c4c-9057-610eff029900.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nPeople are also reading... Richmond police officer Mervin Mayo wows judges on 'America's Got Talent' Richmond's Columbus statue finds new home outside Virginia Michael Gill resigns as superintendent of Hanover schools Why did the Flying Squirrels, not the city, choose Diamond District ballpark builders? Best in Richmond: The winner of the ice cream tournament is ... Gill endured turbulent 9 years as Hanover superintendent EAT Restaurant Partners opening two more restaurants in 2025 Review of city's credit card program shows apparent violations, misuse Body found in Powhatan County creek in area of search for fugitive Sally Bell's Kitchen in Richmond turns 100 years old Preservation leaders question planned demolition of 101-year-old Richmond building Spanberger gets big jump as Democrats look to 2025 Pipe leak that led to James River health advisory began July 2 UPDATE: City scrubs incorrect information on courthouse lockers from website Police ID victim, driver involved in horse-drawn buggy crash\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. {"uri": "8239935060", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-23", "time": "18:20:45", "dateTime": "2024-07-23T18:20:45Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-23T18:20:10Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9019607901573181, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_1c476737-a00b-5bf9-8037-167bba440b30.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nPeople are also reading... Two dead following Sunday night two-vehicle accident in Sioux City's westside Specialty dog boutique store, Urban Mutts, opens in Sioux City Woodbury County Court Report for July 22, 2024 One dead after early Saturday morning collision in Sioux City UPDATED: Okoboji lakes fall below target for lifting boat speeding limits Sioux City substitute teachers to receive extra pay for outside of normal day work State seeks to use Dakota Dunes homicide suspect's confession at trial Jason's Frozen Treats open second dine-in ice cream parlor 2 arrested after I-29 pursuit in Woodbury County High school baseball: Bleeker's walk-off lifts Crusaders over Warriors in substate final Overnight fire closes Spirit Lake Walmart River-Cade crowns 2024 Queen of the River and princesses in Sioux City ceremony Iowa nursing home resident suffocated while trapped in bed rails, state says There's no place like the stage, The New Stage Players presents the Wizard of Oz Woman's body recovered from overturned Jeep in Sac County river\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group.
