"originalArticle": null, "storyUri": "eng-9753637", "eventUri": "eng-9753637", "location": null, "extractedDates": [{"amb": false, "imp": true, "date": "2024-07-18", "dateEnd": "2024-07-21", {"uri": "8239895425", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-23", "time": "17:49:36", "dateTime": "2024-07-23T17:49:36Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-23T17:48:28Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9019607901573181, "url": "https://www.stltoday.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_17a80e4f-b65b-572e-838d-9bb2359e56eb.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, but taking too many meds can cause problems\n\nSwallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nCardinals' TV ratings on record-low pace amid continuing distribution problems: Media Views Tammie Holland, longtime St. Louis radio personality, dies at 53 New St. Louis Public Schools communications chief also lives in Texas Third bear sighting reported in southern Illinois, sheriff says St. Louis Public Schools halts hiring over questions on new administrator from Texas Former St. Louis Rams lineman ordered to pay $500,000 to injured worker O'Fallon, Mo., woman charged with making politically motivated false child sex abuse claim Grades for Cardinals position players: the good, bad and ugly Creve Coeur-based nursing home operator files for bankruptcy O'Fallon, Mo., firm to expand, add jobs with millions in federal dollars for semiconductor push Kohn's Kosher Deli facing lawsuit of nearly $150,000 BenFred: Smart contract stance lets Cardinals be nimble as Goldschmidt tries to salvage season Messenger: A disturbing twist in the case of security guards who tackled BJC patient Ankle injury interrupts Tommy Edman's rehab games, delays his return: Cardinals Extra MSD's recently retired CEO was paid more than $100,000 for unused vacation, sick time\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. The AP is solely responsible for all content.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "8239891879", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-23", "time": "17:46:43", "dateTime": "2024-07-23T17:46:43Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-23T17:45:26Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8941176533699036, "url": "https://pantagraph.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_5c177484-aa8f-51f2-bd70-209f26dbbb6f.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nPeople are also reading... Bloomington teen rebuilds landscape of alma mater for Eagle Scout project Police: Bloomington teen ran from crash before drowning in Illinois River Flick Fact: That J.D. Vance beard and its McLean County significance? Man struck by gunfire early Saturday in north Normal Update: Canton police say missing 2-year-old has been found alive Illinois delegate caught in RNC commotion involving McCarthy, Gaetz Video: Springfield woman ducked, apologized before fatal police shooting Pritzker, Illinois leaders react to Biden leaving presidential race Man held on 9 charges relating to May shootout in Bloomington neighborhood Riverdale man gets 123 years for 2020 murder in Bloomington Flick Fact: The LeRoy man who set an Illinois State Police record? Country singer Trace Adkins to perform in Bloomington this fall McLean County Unit 5 OKs pay hikes for nonunion employees Ronald & Wanda Coyne Bloomington police investigating shots fired Wednesday afternoon on RT Dunn Drive\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nPeople are also reading... Roaming Buffalo Outpost moving from Rutherford Road to Marion's North Main Street 2 teens who died after crash during Greensboro police chase had just finished middle school. A 15-year-old also died. Expanded grocery, new mural: Remodeled Marion Walmart shows off renovations McDowell County man charged with three counts of extortion McDowell alum, WCU pitcher aims for major league draft in 2025 Marion councilman is delegate to Republican convention in Milwaukee Marion's splash pad damaged in act of vandalism McDowell softball player Sage Young commits to Lenoir-Rhyne Avery County woman charged in McDowell for drug crimes Three Lenoir residents charged; More than 400 grams of meth seized McDowell leaders seek land for new animal shelter Art on Hickory oil tanker truck highlights efforts to prevent veteran suicide As police kill Native Americans on this reservation, families left afraid and in the dark What is Soma Church? Hickory pastor talks vision, $4.65 million purchase of former CommScope building McDowell County to host country, bluegrass festivals in August\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. Small meteor creates fireball, loud boom over parts of New Jersey More than just fish and money make Jimmy Johnson's Atlantic City tournament popular Hot meals return to Sister Jean's Kitchen in Atlantic City Ocean City's Night in Venice boat parade is today. Here's what to know.\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. {"uri": "8239899234", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-23", "time": "17:52:29", "dateTime": "2024-07-23T17:52:29Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-23T17:51:23Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8666666746139526, "url": "https://journalstar.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_d6586948-46c1-5b36-b346-3b268410f8f4.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nPeople are also reading... Lincoln restaurant calls it quits after 15 years 'Exhausting, heartbreaking, devastating': Racism at Wayne school pushes family out of Nebraska 'Ready to learn from this': John Cook updates Harper Murray's status for 2024 season Former Nebraska Sen. Ben Sasse to resign as University of Florida president Fleet Farm backed out of northwest Lincoln development This Nebraska couple runs a senior dog sanctuary just outside of Lincoln Recruiting: New Nebraska football staffer aims for pair of 2026 four-star targets Teen accused of causing train derailment near Bennet before recording it Guatemalan chicken restaurant puts Lincoln, Gretna and Crete on its expansion map National retailer confirms plans to open first Lincoln store this fall Is it legal to turn on red traffic arrows in Lincoln? What new Nebraska law says Pedestrian struck by car in west Lincoln dies at hospital, police say Nebraska volleyball's Harper Murray sentenced to probation for DUI Lincoln Animal Control reports first-ever rabies case in a fox 'Death Row:' Nebraska defensive line room embracing new nickname\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. {"uri": "8239914528", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-23", "time": "18:04:37", "dateTime": "2024-07-23T18:04:37Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-23T18:03:20Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8392156958580017, "url": "https://napavalleyregister.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/wellness/too-many-pills-how-to-talk-to-your-doctor-about-reviewing-what-s-needed/article_d6b5bae3-1d7b-546e-8e80-d9b49d2e8cad.html", "title": "Too many pills? How to talk to your doctor about reviewing what's needed", "body": "Swallowing a handful of pills is a daily ritual for many people, from young adults coping with anxiety to older adults managing chronic conditions. Overall, 13% of people in the U.S. take five or more prescription drugs. For those 65 and older, that number is 42%.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems. One pill can lead to a side effect, leading to another pill and another side effect in what experts call a \"prescribing cascade.\"\n\nSome drugs can cause harm if taken for years. Others stop working or interact badly with a new drug. A drug tolerated well at first can cause side effects later, leading to cognitive decline and injuries from falls.\n\nPeople are also reading... Update: Distillery building damaged by fire after Highway 29 crash in Napa Report: Visit Napa Valley staff allege toxic comments, secrecy by CEO Gallagher Vallejo man dies, Napan hurt in Highway 29 crash Napa City Council votes to sell Dwight Murray Plaza, other downtown land to developers Fight between Le Petit Elephant and Alta Heights neighbors gets personal Ira C. Smith, KVON/KVYN radio sports director since 1976, dies at age 88 2023-24 Napa County Boys Soccer Player of the Year: Avi\u00f1a did it all for first-time section champ Calistoga Kevin Courtney, Napa Journal: My street is getting a major luxury upgrade Update: Ridge Fire in Lake County is 15% contained; evacuation orders in effect Four arrested after Napa grocery robbery, police say Customers are raving about Moulin -- downtown Napa's new caf\u00e9 and bakery $400K in unpaid bills for grapes lead to slew of lawsuits against Napa winemaker Napa County acquiring land for flood wall project CHP: K-9 sniffs out 70 pounds of meth during Napa traffic stop Section 1 Little League 10-12 All-Star Baseball: Napa American tops Mill Valley to win title\n\n\"Our metabolism changes as we get older,\" said Dr. Elizabeth Bayliss, who studies deprescribing at Kaiser Permanente's Institute for Health Research in Aurora, Colorado. \"Everyone's ability to metabolize the medications they've been taking for a long time may change.\"\n\nThe nonprofit Lown Institute calls the whole situation a medication overload that will cause 4.6 million hospital visits this decade.\n\nIf your daily pill routine is getting out of hand, ask for a medication review. Here's how to get started with a process called \"deprescribing.\"\n\nAsk for a prescription checkup\n\nStart with a professional you trust, like a doctor or pharmacist, said Lisa McCarthy, a pharmacist and deprescribing expert at the University of Toronto.\n\nIf you're asking your doctor, don't wait until the end of a 15-minute visit. Instead, make a dedicated appointment and tell them when booking you want to talk about your medicines, McCarthy said.\n\nYour doctor may not have the full picture of what you're taking if other prescribers are involved, and some doctors hesitate to manage drugs prescribed by others. That's when a pharmacist can help by reviewing everything and writing up suggestions you can share with your doctors, said pharmacist Bradley Phillips of the University of Florida College of Pharmacy.\n\n\"We're considered the medication experts,\" Phillips said.\n\nCould my medicine be causing a problem?\n\nSwelling, incontinence, restlessness, insomnia -- all are side effects caused by common medications that sometimes get treated with new drugs. McCarthy wants people to ask their doctors a simple question: Could this symptom be related to one of my medications?\n\n\"If we could teach the public to ask that question it would be very powerful,\" McCarthy said. And for a follow-up question, she suggested: Do I still need this medicine?\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | Stitcher | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nPharmacist Barbara Farrell sees dramatic changes in many people she helps at an outpatient geriatric center in Ottawa, Ontario. Some have emerged from a drug-induced dementia after their meds are reduced. A 77-year-old woman was able to leave her wheelchair and walk with a cane after cutting her daily pills from 32 to 17. She had arrived sedated and unable to communicate, and a few months later, she was back to her hobby of knitting.\n\nWeaning off medication takes time\n\nSome meds can be stopped abruptly but others require a slow taper to prevent uncomfortable withdrawal symptoms -- or even life-threatening seizures. Tapering to progressively lower doses is particularly important with drugs for depression, insomnia and anxiety.\n\nAfter two decades on various pills for these conditions, therapist Molly Bernardi of Spokane, Washington, began tapering down her doses.\n\nThe 45-year-old suspected the pills were the cause of her worsening problems with digestion, balance, memory, stiff muscles and flickering dots across her field of vision. When scans ruled out other illnesses, she gradually stopped each of four medications.\n\n\"It's been by far the hardest thing I've ever done,\" Bernardi said.\n\nOne of the final drugs she stopped was the toughest: a benzodiazepine, a class of sedatives that can be harmful if taken long term. Over the course of three months, she used a kitchen knife and a nail file to cut her 1 milligram daily pill into ever-smaller pieces.\n\nShe listened to her body, found support groups on Facebook and used breathwork and prayer to get through withdrawal symptoms.\n\n\"Now when I have a good day, a good hour, a good moment, it's peace and presence like I've never known since before I was medicated,\" Bernardi said. \"I'm just experiencing a little bit of great. And a little bit of great is so great that it keeps me going.\"\n\nBe in charge of your medication list\n\nEven in the best-connected health care systems, McCarthy said, information about prescriptions isn't always shared among doctors. And they won't know about your over-the-counter drugs, vitamins or nightly CBD gummy.\n\nThe only person who knows everything you take is you, McCarthy said. So keep a current list of what you take, why it was prescribed and when you started taking it.\n\nFor an example of a medication list, which McCarthy called a \"tremendously powerful\" tool, look at the workshop materials she and her colleagues designed.\n\nThe Associated Press Health and Science Department receives support from the Howard Hughes Medical Institute's Science and Educational Media Group. For those 65 and older, that number is 42 percent.\n\nIf you're taking multiple meds, it's smart to be aware of potential problems.
